<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13461110346576968715"><em>Man, was a a distant shore, and the men spread upon it in wave... Each wave different, and each wave stronger. </em><br /><br /><em>The Martian Chronicles </em><br /><br />Ray Bradbury is a storyteller without peer, a poet of the possible, and, indisputably, one of America's most beloved authors. In a much celebrated literary career that has spanned six decades, he has produced an astonishing body of work: unf</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13461110346576968715"><em>Man, was a a distant shore, and the men spread upon it in wave... Each wave different, and each wave stronger. </em><br /><br /><em>The Martian Chronicles </em><br /><br />Ray Bradbury is a storyteller without peer, a poet of the possible, and, indisputably, one of America's most beloved authors. In a much celebrated literary career that has spanned six decades, he has produced an astonishing body of work: unforgettable novels, including <em>Fahrenheit 451</em> and <em>Something Wicked This Way Comes;</em> essays, theatrical works, screenplays and teleplays; <em>The Illustrated Mein, Dandelion Wine, The October Country,</em> and numerous other superb short story collections. But of all the dazzling stars in the vast Bradbury universe, none shines more luminous than these masterful chronicles of Earth's settlement of the fourth world from the sun.<br /><br />Bradbury's Mars is a place of hope, dreams and metaphor-of crystal pillars and fossil seas-where a fine dust settles on the great, empty cities of a silently destroyed civilization. It is here the invaders have come to despoil and commercialize, to grow and to learn -first a trickle, then a torrent, rushing from a world with no future toward a promise of tomorrow. The Earthman conquers Mars ... and then is conquered <em>by</em> it, lulled by dangerous lies of comfort and familiarity, and enchanted by the lingering glamour of an ancient, mysterious native race.<br /><br />Ray Bradbury's <em>The Martian Chronicles</em> is a classic work of twentieth-century literature whose extraordinary power and imagination remain undimmed by time's passage. In connected, chronological stories, a true grandmaster once again enthralls, delights and challenges us with his vision and his heart-starkly and stunningly exposing in brilliant spacelight our strength, our weakness, our folly, and our poignant humanity on a strange and breathtaking world where humanity does not belong.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13461110346576968715">...more</a>

      </div>
</div>

<h3 class="communityQuestionSubTitle">
Popular Answered Questions
</h3>
<div id="communityQuestionsWithAnswers">
<div class="communityQuestionAndAnswer">
<div class="communityQuestion">
<div class="questionText">
<a href="/questions/479271-in-2015-is-there-still-anything-relevant">In 2015, is there still anything relevant to appreciate about The Martian Chronicles? I personally found it mired in quaint, unimaginative ideas about what another planet and other intelligent beings might be like, and I wasn't particularly bowled over by its underlying philosophical ideas either.</a>
</div>
<div class="communityQAFooter questionFooter">
<ul class="questionFooterActions">
<li class="footerItem like">
<span id="like_container_community_question_479271" class="likeItContainer"><a href="/rating/voters/479271?resource_type=CommunityQuestion" rel="nofollow" id="like_count_community_question_479271"><span class="likesCount">1 like</span></a> · <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F760921.The_Martian_Chronicles" class="jsLike like_it" rel="nofollow">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp">
<a href="/questions/479271-in-2015-is-there-still-anything-relevant">6 months ago</a>
</li>
<li class="footerItem communityQuestionPermalink">
<a href="/questions/479271-in-2015-is-there-still-anything-relevant">See all 5 answers</a>
</li>
</ul>
</div>

</div>
<div class="allCommunityAnswers">
<div class="communityAnswer">
<div class="communityAnswerBody">
<div class="answererUserIcon">
<a href="/user/show/15526945" class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto"><img src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/users/1355699498i/15526945._UY100_CR19,0,100,100_.jpg" alt="" /></a>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerTextContainer">
<span class="communityAnswererName">
<a href="/user/show/15526945">??????</a>
</span>
<span class="communityAnswerText">
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">This book is nowhere near "what another planet might be like". Mars is Bradbury's symbolism of alien or foreign civilisations. The whole concept is to<a href="#" class="jsShow showAction">…more</a></span><span class="fullContent">This book is nowhere near "what another planet might be like". Mars is Bradbury's symbolism of alien or foreign civilisations. The whole concept is to demonstrate how western civ, deals with people that have a different culture. The main point is that instead of learning from others we try to impose what's right on them. It was a huge deal in the era of modernism and it still is, as the way we treat other countries/civilisation is still modernistic.<br /><br />If you approach it as a sci-fi book, it has nothing to offer, but this is because it is not sci-fi, it is just a chronicle of how America thinks and acts in the present tense.<br /><br />I generally believe that trying to judge the Martian Chronicles as a Sci-Fi and focusing on it's ideas about "Mars", is kind of like judging the Animal Farm as a documentary and discussing it's ideas about life in a "farm". Not the point at all<a href="#" class="jsHide hideAction">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>
<div class="communityAnswerFooter">

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_101061732">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/101061732" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="left imgcol" title="Nataliya"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1395089173p2/3672777.jpg" alt="Nataliya" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/101061732?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 13, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya" class="user" title="Nataliya">Nataliya</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=my-childhood-bookshelves" class="actionLinkLite">my-childhood-bookshelves</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=excellent-reads" class="actionLinkLite">excellent-reads</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=location-is-the-true-protagonist" class="actionLinkLite">location-is-the-true-protagonist</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=books-from-childhood-revisited" class="actionLinkLite">books-from-childhood-revisited</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer101061732">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8823338787429269771"><strong>"<em>We earth men have a talent for ruining big, beautiful things.</em>"</strong><br /><br /><em>The Martian Chronicles</em>, a perfect example of what I'd call a <strong>'quintessential Bradbury'</strong> - fragmentary, at times disjointed, occasionally crossing the line into the realm of surreal, full of his trademark nostalgia and sadness, this account of the <strong>failed American Dream approach to the exploration of the ultimate frontier</strong> never stops fascinating me and drawing me in with its inexplicable charm. <br /><br />(<em>Side note: as a person of Russian descent</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8823338787429269771"><strong>"<em>We earth men have a talent for ruining big, beautiful things.</em>"</strong><br /><br /><em>The Martian Chronicles</em>, a perfect example of what I'd call a <strong>'quintessential Bradbury'</strong> - fragmentary, at times disjointed, occasionally crossing the line into the realm of surreal, full of his trademark nostalgia and sadness, this account of the <strong>failed American Dream approach to the exploration of the ultimate frontier</strong> never stops fascinating me and drawing me in with its inexplicable charm. <br /><br />(<em>Side note: as a person of Russian descent, I reserve the right to run-on long-winded sentences in the best tradition of Tolstoy and Dostoyevsky of which my literature-teacher mother clearly approves</em>).<br /><br /><strong>It is such a multifaceted tale!</strong> It is a condemnation of the dear to the human heart way of 'exploration' and colonization - that is, <strong>coming to a place new to us and attempting to turn it into a carbon copy of 'home', of the place where we come from, of the place that gives us comfort - and all else be damned</strong>. It is an ode to the beauty of the strange and un-understood alienness. It is a criticism of the American Dream which was written in the heyday of this 'Dream'. It is a thinly veiled cautionary tale about the perils of science when misapplied. It is all of the above and none of the above, <strong>with everything masterfully interwoven to create a unique unforgettable reading experience.</strong><blockquote>'<em>Who wants to see the Future, who ever does? A man can face the Past, but to think - the pillars crumbled, you say? And the sea empty, and the canals dry, and the maidens dead, and the flowers withered?' The Martian was silent, but then he looked ahead. 'But there they are. I see them. Isn't that enough for me? They wait for me now, no matter what you say.</em>'</blockquote>The story, for those who somehow are not familiar with it, is simple. In the far future of 1999, rocket ships from Earth start coming to Mars. The Martians - the enigmatic, serene, telepathic race - sense the disturbances. Eventually they die off, and the colonization in the American Dream style begins, until the nuclear war on Earth interferes. But the narrative is not quite this linear. It is made of separate, rather stand-alone short stories that often read as interludes, some straightforward, some surreal, but all of them quite haunting, memorable, and thought-provoking. <br /><br />Bradbury is (was, actually - I still can't believe he's dead) a <strong>master of writing peaceful, nostalgic sadness</strong> that feels upliftingly purifying. His writing is poetic and lyrical, often dreamlike, with almost a musical quality to it. He often straddles the line between cautionary and moralistic, but mostly succeeds at not crossing over to the unpleasantly preachy side. <strong>He is exceptionally good at writing amazing short fiction</strong> - since this is what this book essentially is, a collection of interlinked short stories. He manages to create a memorable, beautifully flowing, sophisticated story without a steadily progressing plot, without a main or even a major character, without even a consistent setting.<blockquote>"<em>Night are night for every year and every year, for no reason at all, the woman comes out and looks at the sky, her hands up, for a long moment, looking at the green burning of Earth, not knowing why she looks, and then she goes back and throws a stick on the fire, and the wind comes up and the dead sea goes on being dead.</em>"</blockquote> <br />=================================<br />Now, as an aside, I heard this book described as 'not really a science fiction book but a <u>speculative fiction</u> book' quite a few times, almost apologetically, as though science fiction is something to be ashamed of. I understand that this book is essentially a crossover phenomenon which appeals to sci-fi fans and 'general public' alike, and describing it as something else besides sci-fi can help generate a wider audience and a broader appeal. <br /><br /><strong>But hey, I realized that I don't want to be the person falling into this trap - the trap of dismissing sci-fi as something that is not literary enough, something of interior quality, something to be apologetic about.</strong> Bradbury, Le Guin, Miéville, Lem (insert your own favorite acclaimed sci-fi author here) are NOT great writers that...ahem...just <em>happen</em> to write sci-fi but maybe not quite really. They are excellent sci-fi writers, and that's how I recommend their books, even at the threat of losing potential audience. After all, Bradbury's <em>The Martian Chronicles</em> was not only one of the first books that I checked out of the 'adult' library, but also the book which cemented my love for science fiction, first fueled by Poul Anderson's <em>Call Me Joe</em>. <br /><br /><em>The Martian Chronicles</em> is an excellent book, the one that I will continue to re-read every few years or so. <strong>It deserves ALL the stars.</strong> <blockquote>"<em>The Martians stared back up at them for a long, long silent time from the rippling water.</em>"</blockquote></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8823338787429269771">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_437001906">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/437001906" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday" class="left imgcol" title="mark monday"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1446402303p2/131922.jpg" alt="mark monday" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/437001906?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 19, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/131922-mark-monday" class="user" title="mark monday">mark monday</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/10480387-the-martian-chronicles" title="Paperback 9780062079930" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=futuristik-classik" class="actionLinkLite">futuristik-classik</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/131922-mark-monday?shelf=rain-man-reviews" class="actionLinkLite">rain-man-reviews</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer437001906">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8576660465521269778"><strong>RIDDLE ME A MARTIAN RIDDLE</strong><br /><br /><br /><br /><em>A Riddle</em>: What walks on two legs, uses two arms, talks like a human, acts like a human, kills humans, replaces humans, wants to be accepted and loved by a human?<br /><br /><em>Answer</em>: A Martian!<br /><br /><br /><br /><em>A Riddle</em>: What walks on two legs, uses two arms, talks like a human, acts like an animal except that's unfair to animals, kills others of its kind, wages war on its own kind, and destroys its own planet?<br /><br /><em>Answer</em>: A Human!<br /><br /><br /><br /><em>A Riddle</em>: What is built like a succession of linked stories, feels at times</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8576660465521269778"><strong>RIDDLE ME A MARTIAN RIDDLE</strong><br /><br />?<br /><br /><em>A Riddle</em>: What walks on two legs, uses two arms, talks like a human, acts like a human, kills humans, replaces humans, wants to be accepted and loved by a human?<br /><br /><em>Answer</em>: A Martian!<br /><br />?<br /><br /><em>A Riddle</em>: What walks on two legs, uses two arms, talks like a human, acts like an animal except that's unfair to animals, kills others of its kind, wages war on its own kind, and destroys its own planet?<br /><br /><em>Answer</em>: A Human!<br /><br />?<br /><br /><em>A Riddle</em>: What is built like a succession of linked stories, feels at times like a play by Brecht, feels at times like a mournful and elegiac ode to the dying of small towns, is a wise tale of human nature, is written with melancholy and sighs, is quietly sinister, is gently tragic, yet is also a science fiction novel?<br /><br /><em>Answer</em>: The Martian Chronicles!<br /><br />?<br /><br /><em>A Riddle</em>: What is a ball of blue fire, a transcended entity, a being that lives in God's grace, a model of wisdom and goodness, and a terrifying symbol of the unknowable? What is meek and shall inherit their earth - but has lost the inclination?<br /><br /><em>Answer</em>: A Martian!<br /><br />?<br /><br /><em>A Riddle</em>: What should have stayed on its own planet? What does not belong on Mars? What persists in persisting? What flees from home? What destroys that home? What flees back to that destruction? What eradicates much of what it comes into contact? What is a hopeless fool? What has a little - just a little - hope for it yet?<br /><br /><em>Answer</em>: A Human!<br /><br />?<br /><br /><em>A Riddle</em>: What is science fiction as parable? What creates a series of haunting and haunted tableaux onto which we can project our own desires and fears? What transcends genre trappings? What is a landscape of forgotten plans and failed goals? What is like a waking dream? What is a journey that begins in death and ends with a small, fragile chance that all is not lost? What is like tears painted on a page? What is witty and sardonic and tender and angry and, finally, full of its own strange and painfully human soulfulness?<br /><br /><em>Answer</em>: The Martian Chronicles!<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8576660465521269778">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1338628971">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1338628971" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/27788046-bookworm-sean" class="left imgcol" title="Bookworm Sean"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1408905793p2/27788046.jpg" alt="Bookworm Sean" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1338628971?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 18, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/27788046-bookworm-sean" class="user" title="Bookworm Sean">Bookworm Sean</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/24924984-the-martian-chronicles" title="Hardcover " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=folio-society-edition" class="actionLinkLite">folio-society-edition</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/27788046-bookworm-sean?shelf=short-stories" class="actionLinkLite">short-stories</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1338628971">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13200877809725062110">For centuries man has dreamed about going to Mars. He has finally achieved this monumental feat, and when he arrived he expected to be greeted as a hero; he expected to be greeted with open arms by the Martians. But, alas, the Martians have a very different opinion to the aliens that invaded their planet. <br /><br /><strong> A funny, and realistic, response </strong><br /><br /><img width="402" height="599" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1437246996p8/1211084.jpg" /><br /><br />When the Earth men arrived the Martians murdered them for a number of wacky reasons. They feared that the invaders would steal their wives and also that the hu</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13200877809725062110">For centuries man has dreamed about going to Mars. He has finally achieved this monumental feat, and when he arrived he expected to be greeted as a hero; he expected to be greeted with open arms by the Martians. But, alas, the Martians have a very different opinion to the aliens that invaded their planet. <br /><br /><strong> A funny, and realistic, response </strong><br /><br /><img width="402" height="599" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1437246996p8/1211084.jpg" /><br /><br />When the Earth men arrived the Martians murdered them for a number of wacky reasons. They feared that the invaders would steal their wives and also that the humans were in fact Martians merely pretending to be humans, this, according to their lore, is one of the many signs of madness. So, the Martians euthanized them. Yes, euthanized them. This made me laugh so much because man, in his naivety, expected an entirely human reaction form an alien race. They couldn’t comprehend that perhaps the aliens may be different to themselves; they didn’t consider that their so called expeditions could be received so negatively. <br /><br /><em>"It is good to renew one's wonder, said the philosopher. Space travel has again made children of us all."</em><br /><br />Indeed, as ever man did not stop to think about what he was doing: he simply rushed in and expected the best. He ignorantly presumed that he wouldn’t be received as a threat and an invader that needed to be fought off. Time and time again man repeats his mistakes and, for me, this formed the main motif of this collection of short stories. Man just never learns. This is signified by the repeated expeditions into the unknown that only ended in disaster, first for the humans and then eventually for the Martian people. <br /><br /><strong> Bradbury questions human existence </strong><br /><br /><img width="400" height="619" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="https://d.gr-assets.com/photos/1437246979p8/1211083.jpg" /><br /><br />A very dim view of humanity is evoked in these pages. Bradbury suggests that Martian culture has transcended its human counterpart; the Martians have accepted an animalistic ethos in which they live for the simple sake of existence. They do not question religion or science; they blend the two together in a display of cultural harmony. However, the brutish man is too limited to do this and as a result has lost all sense of faith. The image of the Martian way of life is captured in the serene beauty of their cities, which is juxtaposed against the humans incessant wondering on foreign soil. <br /><br /><em>"We earth men have a talent for ruining big, beautiful things."</em><br /><br />I think in drawing this parallel Bradbury makes a suggestion on the foolish nature of man. By contrasting the two cultures we see that man will never be fulfilled and complete; he will be harboured by a perpetual longing to have more than he has. The continuous visits to Mars symbolise this. Earth is not enough for man, he wants Mars too. Perhaps I am reading too much into this but I think in this Bradbury creates a message that is strong and true. These short stories come together to provide a picture of man that highlights some of his less admirable qualities.<br /><br />This is a great collection of science fiction stories that, together, speak louder than they do alone. Whilst each is individual they are, of course, meant to be read as a collection. This provides a comment of the nature of man, and a highly entertaining reading experience. <br /><br /><strong>A worthy five stars </strong><br /><br />Ray Bradbury is brilliant! <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13200877809725062110">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/5423" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5-elizabeth" class="left imgcol" title="Elizabeth"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1169594940p2/5.jpg" alt="Elizabeth" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/5423?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 29, 2006</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5-elizabeth" class="user" title="Elizabeth">Elizabeth</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/9624.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Paperback 9780553024401" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=sciencefiction" class="actionLinkLite">sciencefiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=alltimefavorites" class="actionLinkLite">alltimefavorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5-elizabeth?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer5423">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2847097573355696253">I vividly remember reading this book. I was in 8th grade and I read it in Mrs. Zimmerman's class. She was this bizarre ageless woman who wore her jet-black hair in a crusty bee-hive and had gobs of pastel green eye shadow on her eyelids. She also had a rusty voice-like an ex-smoker, and spoke really slowly. She could have been a character in Martian Chronicles. I still kind of wonder if she was human. <br /><br />Anyway, I read this book over and over. There was something so pristine about the world that Br</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2847097573355696253">I vividly remember reading this book. I was in 8th grade and I read it in Mrs. Zimmerman's class. She was this bizarre ageless woman who wore her jet-black hair in a crusty bee-hive and had gobs of pastel green eye shadow on her eyelids. She also had a rusty voice-like an ex-smoker, and spoke really slowly. She could have been a character in Martian Chronicles. I still kind of wonder if she was human. <br /><br />Anyway, I read this book over and over. There was something so pristine about the world that Bradbury creates, and also incredibly odd and mysterious. As a writer, he kind of reminds me of Edgar Allen Poe. <br /><br />Another thing about Bradbury, I heard him speak once in college and he said that he wrote this book from the UCLA library (he wasn't a student there), he just liked to hide out in the basement. He was super eccentric. I liked him immediately.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2847097573355696253">...more</a>

          </span>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/230383896" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="left imgcol" title="Lyn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1428721461p2/5253785.jpg" alt="Lyn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/230383896?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 15, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5253785-lyn" class="user" title="Lyn">Lyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer230383896">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8873052185855783067">Poetic science fiction. <br /><br />Being set in the future and involving space travel, Mars and futuristic technology makes this fit into the science fiction genre, but Bradbury is a writer of literature. This is beautiful writing and Bradbury is an artist with a mastery of the language. <br /><br />Mars could be another dimension, or fairy land, it does not really matter, Bradbury has concocted an alternate reality to explore psychological ethos. If Heinlein is the science fiction ideologist / sociologist, and Clarke</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8873052185855783067">Poetic science fiction. <br /><br />Being set in the future and involving space travel, Mars and futuristic technology makes this fit into the science fiction genre, but Bradbury is a writer of literature. This is beautiful writing and Bradbury is an artist with a mastery of the language. <br /><br />Mars could be another dimension, or fairy land, it does not really matter, Bradbury has concocted an alternate reality to explore psychological ethos. If Heinlein is the science fiction ideologist / sociologist, and Clarke the science fiction anthropologist, and Asimov the science fiction theologist; then Bradbury is the science fiction psychologist. <br /><br />Martian Chronicles is a chronological set of short stories tied together around the theme of Earth colonization of Mars, but it is really about the human psyche and a study of what is best and worst about us. <br /><br /><img width="400" height="300" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1436959546i/15522354._SX540_.jpg" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8873052185855783067">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1001844242">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1001844242" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7213075-ted" class="left imgcol" title="Ted"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1414699107p2/7213075.jpg" alt="Ted" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1001844242?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 11, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7213075-ted" class="user" title="Ted">Ted</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/970651.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553131796" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=lit-american" class="actionLinkLite">lit-american</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=have" class="actionLinkLite">have</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/7213075-ted?shelf=reviews-liked" class="actionLinkLite">reviews-liked</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1001844242">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13764808143391430650">4 1/2<br /><strong>If you want to read a <em>great</em> review of The Martian Chronicles, skip this one and go directly to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/437001906">mark monday’s.</a> Do not pass Go. Do not collect $200.</strong><br /><br />If you’re still here, I will try to keep you entertained for a while by talking about myself, about my reading (and not reading) Ray Bradbury and other SF, about Ray Bradbury himself and his writing, and even a little (near the end) about this book.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[For references, see bottom of review. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><strong>Me the SF fan</strong><br /><br />This summer I decid</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13764808143391430650">4 1/2<br /><strong>If you want to read a <em>great</em> review of The Martian Chronicles, skip this one and go directly to <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/437001906">mark monday’s.</a> Do not pass Go. Do not collect $200.</strong><br /><br />If you’re still here, I will try to keep you entertained for a while by talking about myself, about my reading (and not reading) Ray Bradbury and other SF, about Ray Bradbury himself and his writing, and even a little (near the end) about this book.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[For references, see bottom of review. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><strong>Me the SF fan</strong><br /><br />This summer I decided to re-read the Martian Chronicles (MC). But guess what?<br /><br />It wasn’t a re-read. Nope. Seems, I now believe, that what I’ve been thinking were vague memories of MC must have been vague memories of some other story connected with Mars in some way. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[See “Oh yes …” at the bottom.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I’d always thought of myself as a science fiction fan. Yet, since joining Goodreads a few years ago, hooking up with an ever-growing number of friends, and finding an ever-growing number of SF novels that <em>yes certainly I’ve heard of this probably read it long ago well maybe not but … </em><br /><br />… I’ve come to realize that, like most things in my life, I do not have now, and never have had, a real <em>fan’s</em> deep knowledge of SF. I’m just not that kind of person. My interests are <em>wide</em> (I like to think and hope) but my knowledge in any area is <em>shallow</em> (I generally have to admit). Thus with sf. I was reading it as a pre-teen, then as a teen, was a member of an sf book club, subscribed to Fantasy &amp; Science Fiction (F&amp;SF - the magazine) … then as I transitioned from high school to college, it gradually got left behind for other reading interests, more interesting to a young person beginning to learn about real life.<br /><br /><br /><br /><strong>Ray Bradbury</strong><br /><br />Ray Bradbury was born in 1920 in Waukegan Illinois. Until he was thirteen his family lived there and in Tucson, moving “back and forth” between the two places (Wiki). In 1934 they move to Los Angeles.<br /><br />Bradbury never went to college, due to lack of money. Other than his public school education, he spent major portions of his young life reading in libraries, both in Waukegan and in the LA area. He has said<br /><blockquote>I am a librarian. I discovered me in the library. I went to find me in the library. Before I fell in love with libraries, I was just a six-year-old boy. The library fueled all of my curiosities, from dinosaurs to ancient Egypt. When I graduated from high school in 1938, I began going to the library three nights a week. I did this every week for almost ten years and finally, in 1947, around the time I got married, I figured I was done. So I graduated from the library when I was twenty-seven. I discovered that the library is the real school. (Paris)</blockquote>And what did Bradley read in these thousands of hours spent in libraries? Science Fiction by Clarke, Asimov, Van Vogt, Heinlein – but his greatest loves in this genre were Jules Verne and H.G. Wells; writers such as Steinbeck, Sherwood Anderson, Huxley, Thomas Wolfe, Thomas Mann; women writers like Eudora Welty, Katherine Anne Porter, and Edith Wharton; and poetry – Shakespeare, Hopkins, Frost, Yeats. <br /><br />MC was published three years after Bradbury “graduated”. By this time (1950) his first book (<a title="Dark Carnival by Ray Bradbury" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1794059.Dark_Carnival">Dark Carnival</a>, a short story collection) had been published in 1947 by Arkham House. Bradbury had also published almost 150 short stories in such magazines as <em>Weird Tales</em>, <em>Thrilling Wonder Stories</em> and <em>Planet Stories</em>. His first three stories (unpaid) were published in 1938, two of them prior to his eighteenth birthday. He continued writing during the war years (his bad eyesight prevented him from serving), with 11 stories published in 1943, 19 in ‘44 and 13 in ‘45. From 1946 until the end of the ‘40s between 17 and 20 stories were published yearly. In the second half of the 1940s Bradbury had several stories appear in mainstream magazines: <em>Mademoiselle, Charm, Seventeen, Colliers, Harpers, The New Yorker,</em> and Macleans. <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[(The first three were women’s and girls’ magazines, Macleans was a Canadian news magazine, and the other three were very well-known “culture” magazines, two of them (<em>Colliers</em> and <em>Harpers</em>) founded in the nineteenth century. Most of these magazines are still being published. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br /><br /><strong>Bradbury’s fiction (my version)</strong><br /><br />Following is a list of Bradbury’s books that I knew well (by reputation) at the time in my life when I was sailing away from contact with the shores of SF.<br /><br /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1410363085i/11097516.jpg" /><br /><br /><strong>1950. Bradbury’s first novel.</strong><br /><br /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380321913i/690270.jpg" /><br /><br /><strong>1951, short story collection</strong><br /><br /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1410363085i/11097517.jpg" /><br /><br /><strong>1953 short story collection</strong><br />The title is of course from W. B. Yeats' poem "The Song of Wandering Aengus" (1899):<br />“Though I am old with wandering<br /><br />Through hollow lands and hilly lands,<br />I will find out where she has gone<br />And kiss her lips and take her hands;<br />And walk among long dappled grass,<br />And pluck till time and times are done<br />The silver apples of the moon,<br />The golden apples of the sun.”<br /><br /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1395570680i/9014996.jpg" /><br /><br /><strong>The 1953 classic.</strong><br /><br /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1410363085i/11097518.jpg" /><br /><br /><strong>1955 collection of macabre short stories</strong><br /><br /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380441991i/1236270.jpg" /><br /><br /><strong> 1957 “fix-up” novel. </strong><br />(see following section for “fix-up” novels)<br /><br /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1410363085i/11097519.jpg" /><br /><br /><strong>1962 novel <a title="Ray Bradbury's Something Wicked This Way Comes The Authorized Adaptation by Ray Bradbury" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/9825897.Ray_Bradbury_s_Something_Wicked_This_Way_Comes_The_Authorized_Adaptation">Something Wicked This Way Comes Ray Bradbury</a></strong><br />Fantasy/horror. Stephen King was fifteen. King has stated "without Ray Bradbury, there is no Stephen King."<br /><br /><br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1410363455i/11097583.jpg" /><br /><br /><strong>1969 book of short stories</strong><br />The title of the book (and a short story within) is from the poem of the same name in Walt Whitman’s <em>magnum opus</em> <a title="Leaves of Grass by Walt Whitman" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/27494.Leaves_of_Grass">Leaves of Grass</a>.<br /><br />Of course Bradley didn’t stop writing in 1969. I just lost touch.<br /><br />After letting my F&amp;SF subscription lapse in the 60s sometime, I saw (in the 90s!) that it was still being published, and resubscribed for a few years. I was surprised to see Bradbury stories still appearing in the mag. When he died in 2012 he had published 27 novels, around fifty collections of his hundreds of short stories, over 20 plays, dozens of teleplays and screenplays (including the screenplay for John Huston’s 1956 movie <em>Moby Dick</em>) and several children’s book.<br /><br /><br /><strong>The Chronicles of Mars</strong><br /><br />The Martian Chronicles (1950) was Bradbury’s second published book. If one wants to be technical, it is a <em>“fix-up” novel</em>. According to Wiki, a “fix-up” novel is “a novel created from short fiction that may or may not have been initially related or previously published.”<br /><br />Wiki has a little story about how the book came about, which makes this clear. <blockquote> In 1949, Bradbury and his wife were expecting their first child. He took a Greyhound bus to New York and checked into a room at the YMCA for fifty cents a night. He took his short stories to a dozen publishers and no one wanted them. … Bradbury had dinner with an editor at Doubleday. When Bradbury recounted that everyone wanted a novel and he didn't have one, the editor … asked if the short stories might be tied together into a book length collection. The title was the editor's idea: … "The Martian Chronicles." Bradbury liked the idea and recalled making notes in 1944 to do a book set on Mars. That evening, he stayed up all night at the YMCA and typed out an outline. He took it to the Doubleday editor the next morning, who read it and wrote Bradbury a check for <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span style="display: none" class="spoilerContainer">[<em>here Wiki says “ten thousand dollars”; but the interview cited as the source of the story (ref. Paris) says “seven hundred fifty dollars” – maybe the Wiki writer thought they’d be cute by adjusting it for inflation?</em> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> When Bradbury returned to Los Angeles, he connected all the short stories and that became The Martian Chronicles.</blockquote><br /><br />MC has 26 chapters. Each has a name preceded by a date. For example, the first chapter is “January 1999 : Rocket Summer” and the last is “October 2026 : The Million Year Picnic”. Of these chapters, the copyrights of five (in my edition) are credited to magazine publishers, thus presumably are essentially unedited versions of short stories previously published by Bradbury (1948-1950). Two other chapters are revised versions of previous stories. Altogether these account for about half the Chronicles (by page count).<br /><br />These seven stories have been masterfully worked into a history of man’s colonization of Mars, a satirical story with shifting moods ranging from elegiac, to poignant, to terrifying, to depressing – illustrating mankind’s <em>human, all to human</em> nature. The chronicles are filled with characters who voice the varying human outlooks on everything from interaction with the natives (yes, there are natives), to the idea of remaking Mars in the image of the earth.<br /><br />Beyond this I don’t want to go. It would spoil things for new readers. It’s a short work, which I recommend to almost everyone.<br /><br /><br /><strong>*** Oh yes … that Martian story …</strong><br /><br />My memories of the story I had thought was part of the Chronicles were pretty clear, albeit scant. I felt it had been about a lone man lost and wandering on Mars. So I started hunting through collections of SF short fiction that I still had from long ago. It didn’t take too long to find it, in a book of short stories by A.E. Van Vogt called <a title="Destination Universe! by A.E. van Vogt" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1640223.Destination_Universe_">Destination Universe</a>. When I saw the title <em>The Enchanted Village</em> I knew I had it. It was a story about a man shipwrecked in the Martian desert when the expedition rocket from earth crashes, leaving him as the only survivor. He finds a technologically marvelous, deserted village which seems able to be commanded to produce whatever beings in the village desire. The story draws out the man’s increasingly desperate attempts to make the village understand what he needs. <em>Finally</em> he succeeds … though not quite as he anticipated. Great story. No wonder I kept pieces of it packed in my neurons for half a century and more!<br /><br /><br /><strong>References</strong><br /><br />Bradbury’s Wiki article (<a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Bradbury">_Wiki_</a>)<br />Bradbury’s bibliography on Wiki (<a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Bradbury_bibliography">_ biblio_</a>)<br />2010 Paris Review interview with Bradbury (<a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.theparisreview.org/interviews/6012/the-art-of-fiction-no-203-ray-bradbury">_Paris_</a>)<br /><br <div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_203453310">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/203453310" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="left imgcol" title="Apatt"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1349239948p2/2457095.jpg" alt="Apatt" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/203453310?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 17, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/2457095-apatt" class="user" title="Apatt">Apatt</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=top-20" class="actionLinkLite">top-20</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/2457095-apatt?shelf=sf-top-20" class="actionLinkLite">sf-top-20</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer203453310">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6738085649049503460">Since Ray Bradbury passed away (about a month ago at the time of writing) it occurred to me to reread his books that I have read before, and read the others that I have missed. After rereading <a title="Something Wicked This Way Comes (Green Town, #2) by Ray Bradbury" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/248596.Something_Wicked_This_Way_Comes__Green_Town___2_">Something Wicked This Way Comes</a> last month I thought I'd read <a title="Fahrenheit 451 by Ray Bradbury" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4381.Fahrenheit_451">Fahrenheit 451</a> but as it turned out <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.reddit.com/r/SF_Book_Club">The Reddit SF Book Club</a> chose The Martian Chronicles as book of the month (July 2012) so in order to keep up with the Joneses here we are! How about that for a useless intrro?<br /><br />This book is a fix-up novel which</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6738085649049503460">Since Ray Bradbury passed away (about a month ago at the time of writing) it occurred to me to reread his books that I have read before, and read the others that I have missed. After rereading <a title="Something Wicked This Way Comes (Green Town, #2) by Ray Bradbury" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/248596.Something_Wicked_This_Way_Comes__Green_Town___2_">Something Wicked This Way Comes</a> last month I thought I'd read <a title="Fahrenheit 451 by Ray Bradbury" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4381.Fahrenheit_451">Fahrenheit 451</a> but as it turned out <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.reddit.com/r/SF_Book_Club">The Reddit SF Book Club</a> chose The Martian Chronicles as book of the month (July 2012) so in order to keep up with the Joneses here we are! How about that for a useless intrro?<br /><br />This book is a fix-up novel which is something between an anthology and a novel, and it benefits from both of its sibling formats. The stories are interrelated with only a few recurring characters but read together the whole is certainly greater than the sum of its parts. It is also worth noting that while the table of contents look as if there are almost 30 stories in the book, quite a few of these are not really stories in themselves but brief passages that lead to the next story or provide background information to move the major story arc of the book forward. In general the book tells the story of the colonization of Mars, which in a sense is a little bit like the reverse of H.G. Wells' <a title="The War of the Worlds by H.G. Wells" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8909.The_War_of_the_Worlds">The War of the Worlds</a> in that we invade Mars and they fight back in their quiet ways only to meet the same fate as their counterparts in Wells' book. The major difference is that there is no interplanetary war and it is only the first part of the Chronicles.<br /><br />I just want to make a few notes on the main stories, the brief interludes are also great but too short my noting purposes.<br /><br /><strong>Ylla (February 1999/2030*)</strong><br />A Martian woman dreams (or have a premonition) of an Earthman's arrival. The actual First Contact does not go well.<br /><br /><strong>The Summer Night (August 1999/2030)</strong><br />Name that tune! suddenly an Earth song becomes a hit on Mars but none of the Martians can name it because they pick it up telepathically. The song's lyrics remind me of <em>Stairway to Heaven</em> a bit.<br /><br /><strong>The Earth Men (August 1999/2030)</strong><br />This one starts off as a comical First Contact story, with the Eathmen not getting the rockstar welcome they expected. It soon becomes rather tragic and ends on a dark melancholy note. Wonderful story.<br /><br /><strong>The Third Expedition (April 2000/2031)</strong><br />A little creepy in a nice sort of way, reminds me a little of <em>Invasion of the Body Snatchers</em>. No point noting specially that it is a great story because they all are in this book.<br /><br /><strong>And the Moon Be Still as Bright (June 2001/2032)</strong><br />Us Earthlings do have a tendency to ruin everything we touch with our inconsiderate and uncouth ways. Love that teeth knocking ending!<br /><br /><strong>The Settlers (August 2001/2032)</strong><blockquote>
  <em>most men felt the great illness in them even before the rocket fired into space. And this disease was called The Loneliness, because when you saw your home town dwindle the size of your fist and then lemon-size and then pin-size and vanish in the fire-wake, you felt you had never been born, there was no town, you were nowhere, with space all around, nothing familiar, only other strange men.</em>
</blockquote>I just love this passage, so evocative!<br /><br /><strong>The Green Morning (December 2001/2032)</strong><br />Mirraculous bit of terraforming.<br /><br /><strong>Night Meeting (August 2002/2033)</strong><br />A sort of meeting in The Twilight Zone, feels like a ghost story but is not one. More of a time travelling tale but who is doing the time travelling?<br /><br /><strong>The Musicians (April 2003/2034)</strong><br />Damn kids using Martian bones as xylophones!<br /><br /><strong>Way in the Middle of the Air (June 2003/2034)</strong><br />Wonderful heartfelt story about the Black Americans who have had enough of being lorded over and just want to emigrate to Mars.<br /><br /><strong>Usher II (April 2005/2036)</strong><br />This really does read like a Poe story, or a cross between <em>Fahrenheit 451</em> and <em>Theatre of Blood</em> (old Vincent Price movie).<br /><br /><strong>The Martian (September 2005/2036)</strong><br />Poor little Martian boy. One of the best stories herein.<br /><br /><strong>The Watchers (November 2005/2036)</strong><br />En masse de-colonization.<br /><br /><strong>The Silent Towns (December 2005/2036)</strong><br />A comical story about the last man in the world and the girl he is almost fated to marry. LOL!<br /><br /><strong>The Long Years (April 2026/2057)</strong><br />I like the robo-family.<br /><br /><strong>There Will Come Soft Rains (August 4, 2026/2057)</strong><br />I am not sure if this story is in the public domain (though I doubt it) but the full text seems to get posted online a lot. The first time I read it was as a standalone and I did not really appreciate it. For me reading this story out of the context of <em>The Martian Chronicles</em> does not quite work because I did not know what led up to the abandonment of the automated house at the centre of the story. Now having read the preceding chapters this story is has stronger impact.<br /><br />This is Bradbury at his poetic best.<br /><br /><strong>The Million-Year Picnic (October 2026/2057)</strong><br />Nice optimistic ending.<br /><br />I am useless at deciphering themes but it seems that there is a subtext that we as a species have a nasty tendency to ruin everything, but we are not completely hopeless, if we would only try harder to live in harmony with each other and with nature.<br /><br />Fantastic from beginning to end, and effortless 5 stars.<br /><br />Note:<br />* A 1997 edition of the book advances all the dates by 31 years (thus running from 2030 to 2057) <a rel="nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Martian_Chronicles">(from Wikipedia)</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6738085649049503460">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_6998579">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/6998579" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/248667-christy" class="left imgcol" title="Christy"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1404508394p2/248667.jpg" alt="Christy" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/6998579?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 23, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/248667-christy" class="user" title="Christy">Christy</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/248667-christy?shelf=science-fiction-and-fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction-and-fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/248667-christy?shelf=readinglist2-sf" class="actionLinkLite">readinglist2-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer6998579">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3185414828861127141">Ray Bradbury's <em>Martian Chronicles</em> is a lovely, lyrical collection of short pieces about the human colonization of Mars and its consequences, beginning just before first contact and ending after the death and destruction of most of the population of both Mars and Earth. <br /><br />Since this is a collection of stories and vignettes instead of a novel, the central, guiding element of the book is not a character or set of characters; instead it is the setting and the emotion evoked by Bradbury's prose. His ma</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3185414828861127141">Ray Bradbury's <em>Martian Chronicles</em> is a lovely, lyrical collection of short pieces about the human colonization of Mars and its consequences, beginning just before first contact and ending after the death and destruction of most of the population of both Mars and Earth. <br /><br />Since this is a collection of stories and vignettes instead of a novel, the central, guiding element of the book is not a character or set of characters; instead it is the setting and the emotion evoked by Bradbury's prose. His main concern is Mars itself, as a place separate from human intentions. As one character, a member of an early expedition to Mars, says, "Ask me, then, if I believe in the spirit of the things as they were used, and I'll say yes. They're all here. All the things which had uses. All the mountains which had names. And we'll never be able to use them without feeling uncomfortable. And somehow the mountains will never sound right to us; we'll give them new names, but the old names are there, somewhere in time, and the mountains were shaped and seen under those names. The names we'll give to the canals and mountains and cities will fall like so much water on the back of a mallard. No matter how we touch Mars, we'll never touch it. And then we'll get mad at it, and you know what we'll do? We'll rip it up, rip the skin off, and change it to fit ourselves" (53-4). <br /><br />"There Will Come Soft Rains" takes this emphasis on place over people to its furthest conclusion, perhaps, telling the story of a house that continues running even after its human inhabitants have abandoned it: "The house was an altar with ten thousand attendants, big, small, servicing, attending, in choirs. But the gods had gone away, and the ritual of the religion continued senselessly, uselessly" (167). The house itself takes on life, described as trying to "save itself" when it catches fire (170): "The house shuddered, oak bone on bone, its bared skeleton cringing from the heat, its wire, its nerves revealed as if a surgeon had torn the skin off to let the red veins and capillaries quiver in the scalded air" (171). Bradbury drives this point home with a poem by Sara Teasdale that states, "<em>Not one would mind, neither bird nor tree, / If mankind perished utterly; / And Spring herself, when she woke at dawn / Would scarcely know that we were gone</em>" (169). <br /><br />The book is absolutely suffused with what is best described as a sense of nostalgia for the future. Bradbury romanticizes the Mars of future decades (1999-2026; the book was published in 1950) and distances the reader from Earth itself. Mars is a place of wonders, a place of great civilizations and developed individuals; it is a place to protect. And in <em>The Martian Chronicles</em>, what Mars needs protecting from is us. We bring with us violence, war, advanced technologies that we have yet to master, mining, and ideas about ownership and colonization that, if allowed to spread, would destroy Martian civilization as surely as they destroyed Native American societies, African cultures, and countless other ways of life on Earth. This is a future that has yet to happen, a place we have yet to see, but we, as readers, are meant to sympathize with Martians, with Mars, and root against humans. We are meant to see in conquering humanity the same problems we face here on Earth and in Mars a haven for freethinkers, former slaves, and survivors (c.f., "Usher II," "Way in the Middle of the Air," and "Million-Year Picnic"). This makes <em>The Martian Chronicles</em> both conservative in the way that the nostalgic mode is typically conservative and progressive in its subversive critique of the Earth way of life, specifically the American way of life.<br /><br />In "The Million-Year Picnic," Bradbury's nostalgia for the future turns against human science and scientific progress as the father of a family who has left Earth after the wars to live, practically alone, on Mars, says to his family, "Science ran too far ahead of us too quickly, and the people got lost in a mechanical wilderness, like children making over pretty things, gadgets, helicopters, rockets; emphasizing the wrong items, emphasizing machines instead of how to run the machines. Wars got bigger and better and finally killed Earth. That's what the silent radio means. That's what we ran away from" (179-80). In this anti-technology statement, Bradbury provides little hope for the future of humanity and of Earth and little faith or interest in science. However, elsewhere in <em>The Martian Chronicles</em>, he does afford a small glimmer of hope in a different kind of future, a different attitude toward science, religion, and art. He gives us this in the Martian civilization:<br /><br />"The Martians discovered the secret of life among animals. The animal does not question life. It lives. Its very reason for living <em>is</em> life; it enjoys and relishes life. . . . And the men of Mars realized that in order to survive they would have to forgo asking that one question any longer: <em>Why live?</em> Life was its own answer. Life was the propagation of more life and the living of as good a life as possible. . . . They quit trying too hard to destroy everything, to humble everything. They blended religion and art and science because, at base, science is no more than an investigation of a miracle we can never explain, and art is an interpretation of that miracle. They never let science crush the aesthetic and the beautiful" (66-7). <br /><br />Following this model of science, one that does not separate science and technology from art and religion and humanity, there is hope for a future for humankind. The question Bradbury leaves us with, then, is whether or not we will be able to heed this warning.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3185414828861127141">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_143460217">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/143460217" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/917504-gaijinmama" class="left imgcol" title="Gaijinmama"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1203383974p2/917504.jpg" alt="Gaijinmama" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/143460217?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 26, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/917504-gaijinmama" class="user" title="Gaijinmama">Gaijinmama</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/917504-gaijinmama?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer143460217">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17653206966575755360">Whether you read SF or not, Ray Bradbury writes beautifully. His style is dreamy and lyrical, satirical and funny, and at times creepy as hell.<br />This book is interconnected short stories, rather than a novel in the traditional sense. It describes the imagined human colonization of Mars. Some parts are extremely dated: all the men smoke cigars and shoot things; the women bake gingerbread. I guess cell phones and YouTube were beyond the realm of<br />possibility in 1950, too; Bradbury had people still usi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17653206966575755360">Whether you read SF or not, Ray Bradbury writes beautifully. His style is dreamy and lyrical, satirical and funny, and at times creepy as hell.<br />This book is interconnected short stories, rather than a novel in the traditional sense. It describes the imagined human colonization of Mars. Some parts are extremely dated: all the men smoke cigars and shoot things; the women bake gingerbread. I guess cell phones and YouTube were beyond the realm of<br />possibility in 1950, too; Bradbury had people still using typewriters and listening to the radio. <br />This quaintness doesn't detract from the work's value today, however. I have been haunted since junior high school by "There Will come Soft Rains", near the end of the collection, which describes an automated house that goes on cheerfully making breakfast and vacuuming the carpets, even though the family who lived there, and their entire city, have been obliterated in a nuclear war. <br />I'm 44 now, and it still makes me shiver.<br /><br />I also liked an earlier story in which all the black people in a small Southern town get tired of waiting for civil rights (remember, this was 1950) and simply build their own rocket ships and leave! For that time, Bradbury was making a strong statement, and sadly we still have not ended racism today.<br /><br />I would particularly recommend this book to anyone getting started in the SF genre. The inside front cover of my copy says 14 and up. I'd say that younger kids might not pick up on the political statements, but it would be a great starting point for some interesting discussions, comparing Bradbury's vision of the 21st century with how things have actually turned out.<br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17653206966575755360">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_64566753">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/64566753" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1803452-libby" class="left imgcol" title="Libby"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1248364924p2/1803452.jpg" alt="Libby" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/64566753?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 11, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1803452-libby" class="user" title="Libby">Libby</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1803452-libby?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1803452-libby?shelf=read-in-2009" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2009</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer64566753">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11068311153900926733">I initially gave this three stars but as I wrote my review I moved it down - it's 2.5, really.<br /><br />Taken in historical context I think the book is pretty interesting. It would have been an experience to read it at the time it was written. However, based on where Scifi/Fantasy is now and our current sociopolitical climate, it was just ok.<br /><br />I believe I understand Bradbury’s overall message and goal for the book but in my opinion it was a bit overreaching. His attempt to encompass the entirety of human ex</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11068311153900926733">I initially gave this three stars but as I wrote my review I moved it down - it's 2.5, really.<br /><br />Taken in historical context I think the book is pretty interesting. It would have been an experience to read it at the time it was written. However, based on where Scifi/Fantasy is now and our current sociopolitical climate, it was just ok.<br /><br />I believe I understand Bradbury’s overall message and goal for the book but in my opinion it was a bit overreaching. His attempt to encompass the entirety of human experience regarding Mars exploration and colonization was just too much. The stories tumble around like clothes in a dryer; each making a point or two but without a cohesive structure. The book was effective at points but not the achievement I had expected. <br /><br />What disappointed me most about the novel was the very poor characterization of women. Of course, this must be considered in the cultural climate existing when the book was written, nonetheless, women could have received better treatment. Bradbury choose to include minorities in what he intended to be a favorable light – whether he is succeeded is debatable – why not women? We do see some sympathetic female characters in the stories Ylla, The Off Season and The Million-Year Picnic but Genevieve Selsor in The Silent Towns is just embarrassing.<br /><br />Overall, I see the merit of the work due to its ideas and believe it was a step forward in speculative fiction in its time, but otherwise it was just ok. I will note that I did really enjoy Usher II. It made the book worth reading for me.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11068311153900926733">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
