<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_344752477">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/344752477" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6132231-owlseyes" class="left imgcol" title="Owlseyes"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1451325122p2/6132231.jpg" alt="Owlseyes" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/344752477?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 14, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6132231-owlseyes" class="user" title="Owlseyes">Owlseyes</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Owlseyes by: </span>
      well sians &amp; buryans
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6132231-owlseyes?shelf=sci-fi" class="actionLinkLite">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6132231-owlseyes?shelf=colonization" class="actionLinkLite">colonization</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6132231-owlseyes?shelf=mars" class="actionLinkLite">mars</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer344752477">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3104160958206305340"><br /><br /><br />UPDATE<br />This recent study published in Science*,gives some reason to the imagined Dead Sea of Mars,by Ray Bradbury.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/hx7N4n3"> <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1425670066i/13915484._SX540_.jpg" /></a><br />(NASA scientists have determined that a primitive ocean on Mars held more water than Earth's Arctic Ocean and that the Red Planet has lost 87 percent of that water to space. NASA/GSFC)<br /><br />Prologue<br /><br /><em>Back in the late nineties I was a member of The Planetary Society. I used to receive, at home, their magazine. I always took notice of that name: Ray Bradbury, among the long list of other famou</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3104160958206305340"><br /><br /><br />UPDATE<br />This recent study published in Science*,gives some reason to the imagined Dead Sea of Mars,by Ray Bradbury.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/hx7N4n3">
  <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1425670066i/13915484._SX540_.jpg" /></a><br />(NASA scientists have determined that a primitive ocean on Mars held more water than Earth's Arctic Ocean and that the Red Planet has lost 87 percent of that water to space. NASA/GSFC)<br /><br />Prologue<br /><br /><em>Back in the late nineties I was a member of The Planetary Society. I used to receive, at home, their magazine. I always took notice of that name: Ray Bradbury, among the long list of other famous names as board of directors and Advisory Council members: Carl Sagan (co -founder), Bruce Murray,David Brin, Arthur Clarke …. Maybe I knew one day I would read the Martian Chronicles. <br /><br />And now I had the chance. For some time I still held in my mind the names of the missions (to Mars) and the photos of TPS magazines: Pathfinder and Sojourner roving through the Mars dust and rocks …and then I’ve started reading <u>this idealization of a planet.<br /></u></em><br /><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/OkhuG">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380194i/737017._SX540_.jpg" /></a><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/jS1AM">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380194i/737018.jpg" /></a><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/Rl88K">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380194i/737019._SX540_.jpg" /></a> <br /><br /><br /><br />Bradbury told this story once. He was in San Diego, back in 2001, at Point Loma Nazarene University. He was lecturing about the “hygiene of writing”. He told the students: Christopher Isherwood told him: Aldous Huxley wanted to meet with him; they met and Huxley told Bradbury “you are a poet”. Bradbury got delighted.<br /><br />In an interview he explained how, when he was 29 years old, he went to New York: a journey of 4 days and nights by Greyhound, to get his short stories published. The Martian Chronicles were a collection of separate short stories, but they got together in a tapestry that is the present book (first published in 1950).<br /><br />The Martian Chronicles<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/mKnwKhO">
  <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1419092624i/12987704._SY540_.jpg" /></a><br /><br />The book is a collection of short stories that cover the period of about 60 years of Mars colonization by men,starting in 1999.<br /><br /><br />A place with Blue Mountains,… golden fruits and houses with crystal columns. People (Martians) with gold yellow eyes and brown skin, capable of telepathy (of understanding other languages)… who read on metallic books with salient hieroglyphs. That’s truly poetic. Planet Mars has a Dead Sea…and violet water canals…and twin white Moons. Children play with golden spiders.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/rJcGh">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380194i/737020.jpg" /></a><br /><br />Poetic as well the conversation between Lady Ttt and Mr Iiii and Mr Aaa….on planet Tyrr. From their planet they see Earth as green. But not so poetic for commander Williams,and his men, who conclude later that he’s been visiting a madhouse, and that Martians think “we’re crazy”. This was a story of hallucinations.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/xn6MquE">
  <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1447489270i/17012695._SY540_.jpg" /></a> <br /><br />In another story there’s a character who would like to organize Mars in a way it would resemble Earth;he,himself and fellow men conclude there’s a collective hypnosis: a woman thinking she’s still living on planet earth; a crew member thinks he meets his brother;… and Marylyn. In the end 16 men are dead.<br /><br />Yes, planet Mars harbors a dead civilization. Hathaway, a geologist, concludes: “by the look of their cities it was a beautiful, gracious and philosophical people”. Martians fuse Art and life: not like Americans.<br /><br />When Benjamin Driscoll arrived to Mars there were no trees: he wanted to see a green Mars; the air-like-the-Andes was not satisfactory.<br /><br />By year 2002, 90,000 people arrived …rockets arrived like <br />grasshoppers. Someone says: I must forget earth; I have a lot of fun with the weather here: day hot as hell, and the night cold.<br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/RLLnxCA">
  <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1447488641i/17012578._SX540_.jpg" /></a><br /><br />Martians look like blue spheres. Stone is having a conversation with a priest. Stone had been saved in an avalanche of stones, by the blue lights; the priest says: that proves they have a soul: there’s compassion: they’re not animals. “What kind of Christ they adore?”.<br /><br />A Martian explains: “it’s been 10,000 years… we have abandoned our bodies…we live in happiness, we live in the grace of God…we once were humans, with bodies like you…we live in the mountains and the wind…we have abandoned material life”.<br /><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/ZSeAtmF">
  <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1419094043i/12988031._SX540_.jpg" /></a> <br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/as8sz2E">
  <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1419094246i/12988046.jpg" /></a><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/eQZcU">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380194i/737021.jpg" /></a> <br />(…)<br /><br />And yet in Red City the Martians killed a man.<br /><br />November 2005 news: there’s war on earth. We need to get back. It’s still our homeland.<br /><br />Sam got a territory from the Martians the size of half of Mars. He receives a message from Earth: the Australian continent exploded, London and LA were bombed.<br /><br />Hathaway, a former State governor, says Earth science went ahead of us: wars killed Earth. The governor wants to start a new life on Mars. Hathaway and family are fishing in the Mars canals; and the kids want so badly to see a Martian. Father tells them to look at the image reflected on the waters. <em>Martians are earthlings.</em><br /><br /><a rel="nofollow" href="http://imgur.com/7Zh98">
  <img class="escapedImg" alt="" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380380194i/737022.jpg" /></a> <br /><br /><em>The Bradbury chronicles tell little about Martians, but a lot about humans. </em><br /><br /><br />*<a rel="nofollow" href="https://astrobiology.nasa.gov/articles/2015/3/12/mars-once-had-more-water-than-earths-arctic-ocean/" target="_blank">https://astrobiology.nasa.gov/article...</a><br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3104160958206305340">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1224782464">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1224782464" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7665532-fernando" class="left imgcol" title="Fernando"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449917488p2/7665532.jpg" alt="Fernando" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1224782464?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 20, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7665532-fernando" class="user" title="Fernando">Fernando</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1950540.Cr_nicas_marcianas" title="Paperback 9788445076538" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7665532-fernando?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1224782464">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8167553310208208209">Luego de Farenheit 451, mi libro preferido del viejo Ray. Una distopía interplanetaria que nos muestra el poder de fuego destructivo del hombre, así en la Tierra como en Marte. Bradbury cuenta cómo los colonizadores humanos del planeta rojo van corrompiendo el nuevo hábitat en el que viven: el propio suelo marciano. El capítulo que más me gusta es Usher II, un homenaje de Ray Bradbury a unos de sus más grandes ídolos literarios (que también es el mío también): Edgar Allan Poe.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_65665611">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65665611" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1256262-ron" class="left imgcol" title="Ron"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442983573p2/1256262.jpg" alt="Ron" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/65665611?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 26, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1256262-ron" class="user" title="Ron">Ron</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="did not like it">did not like it</span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="did not like it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1256262-ron?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1256262-ron?shelf=ebook" class="actionLinkLite">ebook</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer65665611">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13475794847879234054">I'm sorry I read this book. It was like watching a 60s Star Trek re-run. Pompous and cheesy fiction. And what little science was offered was wrong.<br /><br />At the time it may have seemed a monumental achievement, but in retrospect, I can see it was garbage. It wasn't science fiction; it was fantasy. Bradbury didn't even get the physics of Mars' moons right, and he should have. No, the science was secondary to him using his stories as a pulpit to preach against the mores and morals of his day.<br /><br />I remember t</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText13475794847879234054">I'm sorry I read this book. It was like watching a 60s Star Trek re-run. Pompous and cheesy fiction. And what little science was offered was wrong.<br /><br />At the time it may have seemed a monumental achievement, but in retrospect, I can see it was garbage. It wasn't science fiction; it was fantasy. Bradbury didn't even get the physics of Mars' moons right, and he should have. No, the science was secondary to him using his stories as a pulpit to preach against the mores and morals of his day.<br /><br />I remember those days--the 50s and 60s--when I'd read folks like Bradbury telling us everything human and American was stupid, dirty and ugly. And I agreed with them. I felt so sophisticated because I thought I was free of the petty middle-class morality and ideas of my parents. I was wrong. I'd just been brain-washed by the petty, snobbish morality and ideas of Bradbury's ilk. When I finally learned to think for myself, of course, I discovered that some of my parents ideas were right, and some of Bradbury's, and some I got from other places. But I had to work a lot of things out for myself.<br /><br />If you're under thirty years old (an arbitrary number) don't despise us older folks who thought twaddle like this was good SF literature. It was all we had. (In comparison Asimov and Heinlein look better now.) And it did start us on the road to better stories and better ideas.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="13475794847879234054">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_449077172">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/449077172" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" class="left imgcol" title="Jonathan"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450116936p2/6313408.jpg" alt="Jonathan" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/449077172?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 27, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/6313408-jonathan" class="user" title="Jonathan">Jonathan</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/10480387-the-martian-chronicles" title="Paperback 9780062079930" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jonathan by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=personal-favourites" class="actionLinkLite">personal-favourites</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=classic-literature" class="actionLinkLite">classic-literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=short-stories" class="actionLinkLite">short-stories</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=fantasy-sci-fi-challenge" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-sci-fi-challenge</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/6313408-jonathan?shelf=classic-challenge" class="actionLinkLite">classic-challenge</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer449077172">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8506816254692371499"><br /><br /><em> <strong>"The way I see it there's a Truth on every planet. All parts of the Big Truth. On a certain day they'll all fit together like the pieces of a jigsaw...For this truth here is as true as Earth's truth, and they lie side by side.</strong> </em>"<br /><br />Ray Bradbury was a great writer whose work speaks volumes (I say this after having read two incredible novels of his, but then you only need one grand novel to touch the reading world). Ray Bradbury was also in my view a poet at heart, if not in his style. Great poets ma</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText8506816254692371499"><br /><br /><em>
  <strong>"The way I see it there's a Truth on every planet. All parts of the Big Truth. On a certain day they'll all fit together like the pieces of a jigsaw...For this truth here is as true as Earth's truth, and they lie side by side.</strong>
</em>"<br /><br />Ray Bradbury was a great writer whose work speaks volumes (I say this after having read two incredible novels of his, but then you only need one grand novel to touch the reading world). Ray Bradbury was also in my view a poet at heart, if not in his style. Great poets make people feel their emotions (it's the reason Sylvia Plath is so great as a poet but also so depressing to read). <br /><br /><em>The Martian Chronicles</em>, like <em>Fahrenheit 451</em> is at times disjointed, bizarre, surreal and fantastical, however it is in all degrees a beautiful novel. Made up of multiple short stories layered chronologically and focused around the connection between Earth and Mars this is science fantasy work at its best.<br /><br />What <em>The Martian Chronicles</em> excels at is in how it takes a foreign idea and expresses familiar ideas. For instance environmentalism, colonisation, the danger of war, alienation, humanity as a collective, religion, society, politics, consumerism and much, much more. I particularly like how he indicates the repetitive nature of history. For such a slim novel Bradbury sure packs in more thought than most of the latest 1000 page epics.<br /><br /><em>
  <strong>“The rockets set the bony meadows afire, turned rock to lava, turned wood to charcoal, transmuted water to steam, made sand and silica into green glass which lay like shattered mirrors reflecting the invasion, all about. The rockets came like drums, beating in the night. The rockets came like locusts, swarming and settling in blooms of rosy smoke.”</strong>
</em><br /><br /><br />I would recommend this book to anyone interested in science fiction or classic writing. Ray Bradbury was one of the great writers of our time and his expression would be missed save that he continues to speak from volumes like this in intricate ways. I do however, recommend anyone stays away from audiobook versions since I found the one I listened to quite confusing, as it did not convey the short story format well.<br /><br />Read this for the ideas, read it for the language, read it because you love to read. Just ensure you read this book at some point. It is a true classic in my eyes.<br /><br /><em>
  <strong>"Is this then how it was so long ago? On the rim of the precipice, on the edge of the cliff of stars, In their time the smell of buffalo, and in our time the smell of the Rocket. Is this then how it was?<br />And she decided, as sleep assumed the dreaming for her, that yes,yes indeed, very much so, irrevocably, this was how it had always been and would forever continue to be."</strong>
</em></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="8506816254692371499">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer14651906642883354604">This book reads more like a series of short stories told in chronological order than it does a novel. Many of the chapters were so powerful and full of such brilliant ideas and insight. Not all, but enough to push this to a 5-star highly recommended level.   <span style="display:none" id="freeText14651906642883354604">This book reads more like a series of short stories told in chronological order than it does a novel. Many of the chapters were so powerful and full of such brilliant ideas and insight. Not all, but enough to push this to a 5-star highly recommended level. There are ideas here that are going to be spinning around my head for months to come. Bradbury manages to cover a huge number of topics in rather a small book. What better platform for examining humanity than exploration to Mars? I can't list all the deepest points touched in this book without giving away spoilers, so pick it up and see for yourself. It's full of twists and turns, excitement and sorrow, you won't be sorry. </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_343809144">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/343809144" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1714947-emily" class="left imgcol" title="Emily"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274968533p2/1714947.jpg" alt="Emily" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/343809144?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 06, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1714947-emily" class="user" title="Emily">Emily</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1714947-emily?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1714947-emily?shelf=classics" class="actionLinkLite">classics</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer343809144">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17422266924734112844">When I heard this morning of Ray Bradbury's death, I went straight to my bookshelf and pulled my old trade paperback copy of <em>The Martian Chronicles</em> out and sat down to re-read it.<br /><br />And it's still just as magical as it was the first time I picked it up and every time since (there have been several).<br /><br />The remarkable quality of Mr. Bradbury's writing is its lyricism. It's almost poetry and it's undeniably beautiful. I stopped to read several sections aloud just because I love the sound of the words Mr.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17422266924734112844">When I heard this morning of Ray Bradbury's death, I went straight to my bookshelf and pulled my old trade paperback copy of <em>The Martian Chronicles</em> out and sat down to re-read it.<br /><br />And it's still just as magical as it was the first time I picked it up and every time since (there have been several).<br /><br />The remarkable quality of Mr. Bradbury's writing is its lyricism. It's almost poetry and it's undeniably beautiful. I stopped to read several sections aloud just because I love the sound of the words Mr. Bradbury put together.<br /><br /><em>The Martian Chronicles</em> is more a loose confederation of short stories than a novel with a single through storyline, but the conflicting themes of our human "talent" for irreverence and destruction, but also our yearning for meaning and purpose and beauty shine through bright and clear. In particular, I love "Usher II". It's so deliciously creepy, and tips the hat to not only his other incredible work, <em>Fahrenheit 451</em>, but Edgar Allan Poe (of course), and L. Frank Baum's Oz books and Alice in Wonderland as well. You don't have too read too closely to hear the echos of Manifest Destiny, the World Wars I and II, and the Cold War with its constant fear of nuclear annihilation. But Mr. Bradbury managed to keep an undercurrent of optimism alive.<br /><br />He will be missed.<br /><br />For more book reviews, come visit my blog, <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.buildenoughbookshelves.com">Build Enough Bookshelves</a>.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17422266924734112844">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_539005124">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/539005124" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3335664-david" class="left imgcol" title="David"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442464595p2/3335664.jpg" alt="David" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/539005124?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Feb 19, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3335664-david" class="user" title="David">David</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6571877-the-martian-chronicles" title="Audiobook 9781433293511" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Earthmen, Martians, cranky old SF authors</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=audiobook" class="actionLinkLite">audiobook</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=aliens" class="actionLinkLite">aliens</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=mars" class="actionLinkLite">mars</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3335664-david?shelf=modern-classic" class="actionLinkLite">modern-classic</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer539005124">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6084388136032189381"><em>The Martian Chronicles</em> has all the virtues and flaws of everything I've ever read by Ray Bradbury. He writes beautiful prose and he's particularly good at spooky and haunting imagery. He's in a different category entirely from other "golden oldie" SF authors — his stuff is deliberately thoughtful and crafted, and tends to be much more human-focused. Even when he's writing "hard" SF, it feels more like a science fantasy, sometimes edging closer to pure fantasy or horror. And you can read all kind</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6084388136032189381"><em>The Martian Chronicles</em> has all the virtues and flaws of everything I've ever read by Ray Bradbury. He writes beautiful prose and he's particularly good at spooky and haunting imagery. He's in a different category entirely from other "golden oldie" SF authors — his stuff is deliberately thoughtful and crafted, and tends to be much more human-focused. Even when he's writing "hard" SF, it feels more like a science fantasy, sometimes edging closer to pure fantasy or horror. And you can read all kinds of metaphors into his work, often metaphors completely different from the ones he intended, if his reaction to interpretations of <em>Fahrenheit 451</em> is anything to go on.<br /><br /><em>The Martian Chronicles</em> is, as the title suggests, more a themed collection of short stories than a novel, chronicling humanity's exploration and exploitation of Mars. It starts with the first ill-fated Mars expedition, when the first Earthman on Mars is greeted by a Martian roused to jealousy by his dissatisfied wife's clairvoyant dreams, and continues through the inevitable follow-up expeditions and colonization, in which the Martians all but disappear, becoming ghosts haunting their own planet, and humanity brings its troubles and all its baggage to Mars. The final stories, in particular, after a war destroys life back on the home planet, are eerie, with vivid descriptions of robot houses on Earth still cooking breakfast for families that were long ago atomized in a nuclear war, and a few lone survivors trekking through the ghost towns and dry canals of Mars. There were parts of this book that were truly marvelous and timeless.<br /><br />That said - the flaws. Ray Bradbury, like so many of his generation, writes like a cranky old white man and he always has. He seems unable to conceive of a family, a society, or a civilization that doesn't resemble Middle America circa 1950, when <em>The Martian Chronicles</em> was published. Even the Martians, despite their elegiac voices and physical descriptions — brown skin, copper eyes, psychic powers, and evolution into non-corporeal bodies — are first introduced to us as a bored married couple following behavioral tropes that would not be out of place in a 1950s sitcom. The Martian household is imaginatively described, with its magnetic dust to clean and its fire chariot for transportation and the mask worn by a Martian man going out to hunt, with his rifle firing bee-like cartridges, but it's essentially a Flintstones or Jeffersons-like mapping of suburban America onto an alien landscape.<br /><br />That said, Earthmen behaving exactly as they did back on Earth, and trying to remake Mars in the image of Smalltown, America, was no doubt part of the point. <em>The Martian Chronicles</em> shows Earthmen ruining everything, like they always do, Mars being no exception.<br /><br />This is a classic that deserves a good read, and there is a timeless quality about it, but there's also a datedness in Bradbury's characterization, an ability to imagine and illustrate themes beautifully but not characters, all of whom are as stereotypical and whitebread as those you'd have found on TV at the time of the book's writing.<br /><br />3.5 stars for superior prose and imagination and vision, but dated tropes and characters who are simply mouths to voice ideas.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6084388136032189381">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1081466121">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1081466121" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/10743961-rob" class="left imgcol" title="Rob"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1422270416p2/10743961.jpg" alt="Rob" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1081466121?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 12, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10743961-rob" class="user" title="Rob">Rob</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/22782036-the-martian-chronicles" title="Audio CD 9781491584583" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=audio-book" class="actionLinkLite">audio-book</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=sff-audio" class="actionLinkLite">sff-audio</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=read-2015" class="actionLinkLite">read-2015</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=stand-alone" class="actionLinkLite">stand-alone</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1081466121">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2570549449580058577"><strong>Executive Summary:</strong> My first by Mr. Bradbury, but won't be my last. Then again since I'll be reviewing <a title="Something Wicked This Way Comes (Green Town, #2) by Ray Bradbury" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/248596.Something_Wicked_This_Way_Comes__Green_Town___2_">Something Wicked This Way Comes</a> for SFFAudio later this month..that was probably going to happen anyways. :-D<br /><br /><strong>Audio book:</strong> Mark Boyett's voice reminds me a bit of Rod Serling, which as I get into a bit below seemed a perfect fit. I know there are multiple versions of the audiobook. I'm not sure how easy they are to get a hold of, but this one seems like a good option.<br /><br /><strong> <u>Full Review</u> </strong><br />I've never read a</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2570549449580058577"><strong>Executive Summary:</strong> My first by Mr. Bradbury, but won't be my last. Then again since I'll be reviewing <a title="Something Wicked This Way Comes (Green Town, #2) by Ray Bradbury" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/248596.Something_Wicked_This_Way_Comes__Green_Town___2_">Something Wicked This Way Comes</a> for SFFAudio later this month..that was probably going to happen anyways. :-D<br /><br /><strong>Audio book:</strong> Mark Boyett's voice reminds me a bit of Rod Serling, which as I get into a bit below seemed a perfect fit. I know there are multiple versions of the audiobook. I'm not sure how easy they are to get a hold of, but this one seems like a good option.<br /><br /><strong>
  <u>Full Review</u>
</strong><br />I've never read anything by Mr. Bradbury before. I'm not really well read in the "classics". There is too much modern stuff I want to read, and in general I prefer fantasy to Sci-Fi. But when Brilliance Audio was releasing some of his better known works on Audio CD (although the production itself was done by Audible) last year, I jumped at the chance to finally give him a try.<br /><br />I've been in a bit of a reading funk this year, and was trying to figure out what to read AFTER this book to get me out of it. Since it was short though, I wanted to listen to it sooner rather than later, write up my review then move onto something else.<br /><br />Apparently I just needed to listen to this. Apart from one story (Way in the Middle of the Air) which made me really uncomfortable and showed it's age. It appears to have been eliminated from several of the more recent editions of this book, and I wish I had skipped it as it really adds very little to this collection.<br /><br />Everything else was enjoyable. A bit depressing, but enjoyable. Mr. Bradbury paints a bleak picture of a future that thankfully never came. This isn't hard sci-fi by any means, but more like dystopian space opera.<br /><br />I would have never thought something bleak would lighten my mood, but the stories were that good, and the prose are excellent. They reminded me a lot of the Twilight Zone, although I know these stories predate that show. I think The Silent Towns could easily have been an episode of the show, as could several others.<br /><br />I think my favorite of the collection is Usher II. I can't pretend to get all the references apart from Poe and Lovecraft, but his tale of revenge for censorship is quite good. I'll have to check out the Poe story <a title="The Fall of the House of Usher by Edgar Allan Poe" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/175516.The_Fall_of_the_House_of_Usher">The Fall of the House of Usher</a> that seems to have influenced it.<br /><br />Overall this is an excellent collection of stories, and if like me you haven't read it/anything by Mr. Bradbury, this seems like as good a place as any to start.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2570549449580058577">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_44496071">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/44496071" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1754850-oscar" class="left imgcol" title="Oscar"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1248783057p2/1754850.jpg" alt="Oscar" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/44496071?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 02, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1754850-oscar" class="user" title="Oscar">Oscar</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/1867754.Cr_nicas_Marcianas" title=" 9789505470006" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1754850-oscar?shelf=m%C3%ADos" class="actionLinkLite">míos</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1754850-oscar?shelf=5-estrellas" class="actionLinkLite">5-estrellas</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1754850-oscar?shelf=ciencia-ficci%C3%B3n" class="actionLinkLite">ciencia-ficción</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1754850-oscar?shelf=fix-up" class="actionLinkLite">fix-up</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1754850-oscar?shelf=edad-de-oro" class="actionLinkLite">edad-de-oro</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1754850-oscar?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer44496071">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3582509743842542448">'Crónicas Marcianas' es una novela que deja un cierto sabor nostálgico, y en el que la soledad de la Humandidad posee un papel preponderante. La visión de ese Marte mítico, poético y fantasmagórico es imborrable.<br /><br />El libro es un <em>fix up</em>, lo que los expertos llaman relatos independientes con un hilo argumental y pensonajes en común que los conectan, formando un todo, una novela por sí misma. Los relatos fueron publicados a finales de la década de los 50, concretamente entre 1946 y 1950, en diferente</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3582509743842542448">'Crónicas Marcianas' es una novela que deja un cierto sabor nostálgico, y en el que la soledad de la Humandidad posee un papel preponderante. La visión de ese Marte mítico, poético y fantasmagórico es imborrable.<br /><br />El libro es un <em>fix up</em>, lo que los expertos llaman relatos independientes con un hilo argumental y pensonajes en común que los conectan, formando un todo, una novela por sí misma. Los relatos fueron publicados a finales de la década de los 50, concretamente entre 1946 y 1950, en diferentes revistas, siendo tal su éxito que en seguida se publicó como libro.<br /><br />La historia de 'Crónicas Marcianas' trata la colonización del planeta por parte de los humanos, donde es inevitable comparar con la pasada conquista del oeste por parte de los primeros pioneros americanos. La novela está muy bien estructurada, y en los primeros capítulos o relatos conocemos a los marcianos, y, posteriormente, cómo se enfrentan éstos con los humanos de las primeras expediciones, que ha llegado en cohetes. En los capítulos siguientes se habla de los asentamientos y de sus consecuencias, pero de ésto es mejor no hablar demasiado.<br /><br />Una cosa ha de quedar clara, aquí no se encuentran hombrecitos verdes y batallas con láseres. Tampoco se habla de elementos tecnológicos y descripciones de extraños artefactos. Parece que Bradbury quiso eludir intencionadamente toda parafernalia tecnológica y centrarse en las relaciones humanas. Fue ésto mayoritariamente lo que atrajo a la crítica no especializada en ciencia ficción hacia este libro, además de la calidad de la prosa del autor. Muchos detractores del género tienen a esta novela como una de sus favoritas, quizá por esta ausencia de elementos técnicos, que convierten a la obra en intemporal.<br /><br />Sin lugar a dudas, 'Crónicas Marcianas' es un gran libro, donde el tono poético y épico, e incluso terrorífico, son primordiales. Esas escenas de paisajes desérticos y bucólicos, con las ciudades marcianas abandonadas, allá en la lejanía, en las montañas azules, y un personaje mirando hacia arriba, observando ese planeta diminuto llamado Tierra, son memorables.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3582509743842542448">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10494582579762737204">Another one read for my Coursera SF/F class. As usual when I've just finished a book, I have no idea what I'm going to write my essay about, but I have one day left to figure it out...<br /><br />The thing that interests me most, I guess, is that Mars colonises the colonisers. In different ways in different vignettes, but it's there -- particularly in that last chapter/section. In a sense it feels like a recent book: the commentary on the spoiling of the world, and on colonisation; in others it feels so dat</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10494582579762737204">Another one read for my Coursera SF/F class. As usual when I've just finished a book, I have no idea what I'm going to write my essay about, but I have one day left to figure it out...<br /><br />The thing that interests me most, I guess, is that Mars colonises the colonisers. In different ways in different vignettes, but it's there -- particularly in that last chapter/section. In a sense it feels like a recent book: the commentary on the spoiling of the world, and on colonisation; in others it feels so dated -- the treatment of people of colour, women, the obsession with nuclear war (which is still an issue, but not the same kind of deep-seated fear, I think)... <br /><br />The science itself (how long it might take to fly to Mars, being the obvious example) isn't really important to the story/themes: it's there as a backdrop, not at all used in the way H.G. Wells used science.<br /><br />As with most of Bradbury's work which I've come across so far, there are some gorgeous sections of prose here, and it's all very well crafted and easy to read, as you'd expect.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_29090645">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/29090645" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1390884-greg-heaney" class="left imgcol" title="Greg Heaney"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1373169801p2/1390884.jpg" alt="Greg Heaney" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/29090645?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 19, 2010</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1390884-greg-heaney" class="user" title="Greg Heaney">Greg Heaney</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1390884-greg-heaney?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1390884-greg-heaney?shelf=favorites" class="actionLinkLite">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer29090645">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15446834875073355607">The Martian Chronicles is like a magic trick: the more times I read it, the better it gets. It is wonderful, beautiful, moving, and heartbreaking unlike any other science fiction novel I’ve ever read, possibly any novel of any genre. Sure, it’s going on 60 years old. The technological inconsistencies with today’s world of space flight are a product of that. But, in short, it just doesn’t matter. Like all good novels, The Martian Chronicles aren’t about what the title and cover illustration show.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15446834875073355607">The Martian Chronicles is like a magic trick: the more times I read it, the better it gets. It is wonderful, beautiful, moving, and heartbreaking unlike any other science fiction novel I’ve ever read, possibly any novel of any genre. Sure, it’s going on 60 years old. The technological inconsistencies with today’s world of space flight are a product of that. But, in short, it just doesn’t matter. Like all good novels, The Martian Chronicles aren’t about what the title and cover illustration show. The science fiction is just a medium Bradbury uses to reach something incredibly basic about human nature and our need to grow, expand, build and destroy. And yes, that destruction is usually self-centered. Perhaps it is the guilt that we feel about our own need for space. Perhaps it is the genuine anger we feel towards those who interrupt our manifest destiny. Perhaps it’s all a tug of war between the part of us that wants so terribly to be left alone and the part that would rather die than face loneliness. Either way, The Martian Chronicles are a testament of our human nature.<br /><br />The underlying theme between each individual episode of the Chronicles is our almost complete inability to deal with our differences. Even those who attempt to embrace the foreignness of Mars ultimately give it up for home again. No amount of human intervention or understanding can bridge the gap between the Martians and us, the aliens. Diplomacy fails, communication is nearly impossible; murder leads to genocide. And still their ghosts haunt us. Even within humanity, the gaps are too wide. We still murder each other, we still fight for power, for land, for cultural supremacy. Mars, the great equalizer, the modern American West, cannot make peace prevail.<br /><br />There is a somberness to this novel that I’ve felt very few other places. I think readers of Tolkien’s novella Smith of Wooton Major, or even Roverandom, will know what I’m talking about. Mars is a beautiful land, breathtaking, really. The Martian ruins (are they truly “ruins” at all?) have a pull that places on Earth do not. And yet, for all of its glory, it is a dead land. It is past its prime, and humanity will never be able to see Mars for what is was, or for what it should have been. Bardbury’s Mars is a place that I long to know, to see, just as other novels have characters that we long to meet and become friends with. Like Smith or Rover, I feel a real sense of sadness, bereavement all most, at leaving Mars forever. Bradbury makes us want to stay on Mars and experience it for ourselves. That, I believe, is something few writers accomplish. I feel sadness at leaving a fictional place and fictional characters behind. That is true talent.<br /><br />This is a keystone work of science fiction, of Bradbury’s work, and Americana. It cannot be passed up, for its cultural significance as well as literary. I cannot properly express my love for this book in words. The only way for me to explain it is to beg you to read The Martian Chronicles and feel it for yourself. I promise you, you will never look up at the sky in the same way again.<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15446834875073355607">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_18226458">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18226458" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/903390-werner" class="left imgcol" title="Werner"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1216337931p2/903390.jpg" alt="Werner" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/18226458?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 05, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/903390-werner" class="user" title="Werner">Werner</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Science fiction fans</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/903390-werner?shelf=science-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">science-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer18226458">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2469842095445173742">Though the 16 stories that comprise this collection are fitted into a super-imposed chronological framework, and are joined by some short units of bridging material, they were originally composed as stand-alones, not part of any larger unity. Bradbury was primarily a writer of short fiction, the main medium for his characteristic supernatural and science fiction in the era when he started writing; this book simply collects most of the stories he composed in the 1940s set on, or related to, Mars.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2469842095445173742">Though the 16 stories that comprise this collection are fitted into a super-imposed chronological framework, and are joined by some short units of bridging material, they were originally composed as stand-alones, not part of any larger unity. Bradbury was primarily a writer of short fiction, the main medium for his characteristic supernatural and science fiction in the era when he started writing; this book simply collects most of the stories he composed in the 1940s set on, or related to, Mars. Several of them have totally conflicting or contradictory premises and features, and they vary wildly in tone and effect. (They're uneven in quality as well, as noted below.) But that said, there are certain recurrent themes that bind them. Bradbury envisioned Mars colonization as a kind of re-enactment of the settling of the American frontier, a new New World with the same pitfalls and the same potential promise. He also was haunted, as were most post-World War II SF writers working in the long shadow of Hiroshima and Nagasaki, by the threat of nuclear war, and that concern is reflected in a number of the stories.<br /><br />Some reviewers, both on and off of Goodreads, have faulted Bradbury for a drastic lack of scientific accuracy in his portrayal of Mars, which he pictures as much more hospitable to humanoid life than it actually is. (His ascription to his Martians of psi powers --the possibility of which, to say the least, is undemonstrated-- also doesn't please hard SF purists.) To a degree, those criticisms miss the point, however: Bradbury isn't trying to write scientifically accurate, hard SF, and failing at it; rather, he's writing from the standpoint of the genre's "soft" tradition (of which he was always an exponent, even in the days when the U.S. pulp SF ghetto was rigidly dominated by the hard school). He simply posited the kind of Mars he wanted for the kind of stories he wanted to tell, knowing full well that it was fictional and invented; if we take his "Mars" on those terms, the stories work as he intended.<br /><br />Criticism has also been directed at these stories on the grounds of an alleged anti-American agenda. His space explorers/colonists are all Americans; they invade and occupy Mars, inadvertently bringing disease germs that virtually annihilate the native Martians, who are portrayed in "Ylla" as an aesthetic, artistic race. "Way in the Middle of the Air" is openly critical of white racism in the segregated U.S. South of the 1940s. And several of the stories posit a nuclear war on Earth, with the penultimate story, "There Will Come Soft Rains," graphically portraying the wanton total destruction of life and negation of human science and achievement that such a war would entail. These features, however, do not add up to or prove a root-and-branch essential hostility to America and its values. (Bradbury is actually a product of a small-town America that he often evokes with an affectionate nostalgia that's obviously genuine.) The parallel between the fate of Bradbury's Martians and our Indians is real and historically grounded; you can't re-tell American frontier history without facing it --and at least here, the Martians die only of unintentionally-borne disease; they aren't victims of deliberate genocide. (It could also be questioned whether the portrayal of Martian attitudes is intended as glowingly positive --Yll, as his wife recognizes, is a cold-blooded xenophobe and murderer.) But the promise of the frontier as a place of new beginnings, new possibilities and a second chance is also evoked here; one could view that as a positive take on the meaning of the American experience. Criticism of the treatment by some Americans of blacks (who are also Americans) isn't in itself anti-American; it echoes the sentiment of the song "My Country, "Tis of Thee," where it says, "God mend thine every flaw." And to view nuclear war as immoral idiocy is not a position of disloyalty to America or American principles, unless we assume that mass genocide and mass suicide have always been intrinsic American ideals (they haven't). "Usher II" expresses a libertarian cultural attitude that's arguably quintessentially American; and "The Million-Year Picnic" brings a family of American nuclear war survivors to Mars as agents of a new beginning, where they finally have a chance "get it right."<br /><br />There are certainly merited criticisms that can be made of several of these stories. The only one with any religious message, "Fire Balloons," is simply a wooden preaching of the "gospel" according to Gnosticism: "salvation" through evolving away from icky physicality. (The apostles Paul and John, judging from their letters, would have puked over it. :-)) The conclusion (not the climax) of "Mars Is Heaven!" is supposed to be dramatically effective, but doesn't make sense in the context. IMO, the poorest story in the group is "The Silent Towns," which showcases the sexism of Bradbury's generation at its worst; it has no message, except ridicule of overweight females and an attempt to generate "humor" at their expense.<br /><br />Overall, though, I liked this collection; obviously, some stories are better than others, but I thought that most worked artistically. For me, Bradbury's style is a plus; it's lyrical and evocative, and full of appeals to all of the senses. My favorites in the group are "There Will Come Soft Rains" and "Way in the Middle of the Air," which I think are masterpieces. (Either would have been better selections for the Science Fiction Hall of Fame, IMO, than "Mars Is Heaven!").</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2469842095445173742">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer957793436">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4343654735653510210">.<br /><br />I've seen this referred to as a masterpiece of science fiction, but it's less about the science and more about the faults and failures of humanity, in this case Americans. He delivers a sharp slap to the face of American racial prejudice, aggressive colonization, wastefulness and disregard of the environment. I think Bradbury would be shocked to see the same conditions existing in the 21st century. He would also be shocked to see we haven't sent any humans to Mars yet.<br /><br />This is a collection of sh</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4343654735653510210">.<br /><br />I've seen this referred to as a masterpiece of science fiction, but it's less about the science and more about the faults and failures of humanity, in this case Americans. He delivers a sharp slap to the face of American racial prejudice, aggressive colonization, wastefulness and disregard of the environment. I think Bradbury would be shocked to see the same conditions existing in the 21st century. He would also be shocked to see we haven't sent any humans to Mars yet.<br /><br />This is a collection of short stories that taken as a whole has the appearance of a novel. It very much reminds me of Sherwood Anderson's <a title="Winesburg, Ohio by Sherwood Anderson" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/80176.Winesburg__Ohio">Winesburg, Ohio</a>, which he credits with influencing the structure of Chronicles. Another contemporary book using this technique is Elizabeth Strout's <a title="Olive Kitteridge by Elizabeth Strout" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/1736739.Olive_Kitteridge">Olive Kitteridge</a>. Bradbury's picture of Mars and Martian people also remind me of Edgar Rice Burrough's vision in his <a title="A Princess of Mars (Barsoom, #1) by Edgar Rice Burroughs" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/40395.A_Princess_of_Mars__Barsoom___1_">A Princess of Mars</a><br /><br />I'm sure many readers have avoided The Martian Chronicles because it is mid-twentith century science fiction, but Bradbury rises above the cliches' of the genre to offer a view of who we are and what we need to change before introducing ourselves to future "Martians".</span>

  <a href="/user/show/1628041-book-concierge" class="left imgcol" title="Book Concierge"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1324904606p2/1628041.jpg" alt="Book Concierge" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1496484830?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 05, 2016</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1628041-book-concierge" class="user" title="Book Concierge">Book Concierge</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=concierge" class="actionLinkLite">concierge</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=library" class="actionLinkLite">library</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=short-stories" class="actionLinkLite">short-stories</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1628041-book-concierge?shelf=audio" class="actionLinkLite">audio</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1496484830">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17505738026460767230">Audiobook narrated by Stephen Hoye<br /><br />Bradbury’s first published book (1950) is an anthology of connected short stories centering on men escaping an Earth about to be destroyed by war for a chance at a new beginning on the red planet. <br /><br />I first read this in about 1962 and it remains a favorite. It captured my imagination with vivid imagery and fantastic settings, but recognizable scenarios. It was a time when we feared “the red menace” (i.e. Communists), when racial tensions were rising, and when neig</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText17505738026460767230">Audiobook narrated by Stephen Hoye<br /><br />Bradbury’s first published book (1950) is an anthology of connected short stories centering on men escaping an Earth about to be destroyed by war for a chance at a new beginning on the red planet. <br /><br />I first read this in about 1962 and it remains a favorite. It captured my imagination with vivid imagery and fantastic settings, but recognizable scenarios. It was a time when we feared “the red menace” (i.e. Communists), when racial tensions were rising, and when neighbors built bomb shelters in case an atom bomb was dropped. It was already twelve years old when I first read it, yet Bradbury’s stories reflected the time period when I was reading it, even though it was set in the far future (1999-2026). <br /><br />One small story is the one that stayed with me … a lone man whose purpose is to plant trees to help oxygenate the Martian air for the coming Earthmen who will colonize the planet. So when I saw the trailer for the movie based on Andy Weir’s <a title="The Martian by Andy Weir" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/18007564.The_Martian">The Martian</a>, and saw the small little plant growing … well, I immediately thought of Bradbury’s classic and decided to re-read it. <br /><br />I’m so glad I did. My adult self has a different perspective than the child I was when I first read it. I’m struck by the larger themes – colonization, war, environmental damage, racial tensions – and less by the excitement of imagining this new world. <br /><br />The text edition I have has the original stories and timeframe. A 1997 edition of the book updates the timeframe by 31 years to 2030-2057, and switches out some of the original stories for others which are less dated. (<em>Way in the Middle of the Air</em> was deleted in favor of <em>The Wilderness</em>.) This eliminates the odd sensation of reading about a “future” that is already in the past, but the story arc remains the same. <br /><br />My rating is based in part on my memory of my first reaction when I read it back in 1962. Had I rated it solely based on listening to the audio version, I would have given it only 3 stars.<br /><br />Stephen Hoye does a fine job narrating the audiobook, which is based on the newer edition. But there is something about his voice that just irritated me in this performance, and lessened my enjoyment. I stopped listening about half-way through and finished by reading the text. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="17505738026460767230">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_582512784">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/582512784" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9456990-stuart" class="left imgcol" title="Stuart"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1452041473p2/9456990.jpg" alt="Stuart" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/582512784?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 24, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9456990-stuart" class="user" title="Stuart">Stuart</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/76778.The_Martian_Chronicles" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780553278224" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=literature" class="actionLinkLite">literature</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=fantastic-weird" class="actionLinkLite">fantastic-weird</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=classic-sf" class="actionLinkLite">classic-sf</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=dying-earth-far-future" class="actionLinkLite">dying-earth-far-future</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9456990-stuart?shelf=alien-contact" class="actionLinkLite">alien-contact</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer582512784">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10721439480568807169"><strong>The Martian Chronicles: A meditation on failed American ambitions</strong><br />(Revised and expanded after listening to audio version by Scott Brick)<br />Originally posted at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com">Fantasy Literature</a><br />I really didn’t like this book when I first read it last year. Considering its legendary status in the genre and its very high ratings by other reviewers I respect, I was really looking forward to finally reading this classic SF tale. But what I discovered was a series of loosely-connected vignettes with some connecting mate</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10721439480568807169"><strong>The Martian Chronicles: A meditation on failed American ambitions</strong><br />(Revised and expanded after listening to audio version by Scott Brick)<br />Originally posted at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.fantasyliterature.com">Fantasy Literature</a><br />I really didn’t like this book when I first read it last year. Considering its legendary status in the genre and its very high ratings by other reviewers I respect, I was really looking forward to finally reading this classic SF tale. But what I discovered was a series of loosely-connected vignettes with some connecting material that seemed fairly superfluous. While I found the first few stories actually featuring Martians very well written and intriguing, once the Martians went offstage and were replaced by an endless series of annoying, hokey Midwesterners from 1950s America, my interest died more quickly than the Martians themselves. <br /><br />However, I knew I must be missing something. This is considered one of the greatest works of mid-20th century science fiction, and is highly regarded even by the literati outside the genre. So I decided to try the audiobook version, which is narrated by Scott Brick (there are several versions available on Audible). I’m glad I did.<br /><br />Ray Bradbury’s lyrical, melancholy, poetic writing is ideally suited to narration with the right voice. The stories take on added emotional weight when you can hear his beautiful descriptions of the Martian landscape, empty crystal cities, rocket ships lifting off from Earth, and the silent viewing of nuclear holocaust from space. The imagery is powerful, and the events of the stories are humorous, tragic, melancholy, and ironic by turns.<br /><br />I think I understood the themes of the stories better as well. Initially I thought Bradbury was again wallowing in nostalgia for a long-lost Midwestern America of the 1920s (like he did in Dandelion Wine), particularly in stories such as “The Earth Men”, “The Third Expedition”, “The Martian”, “The Long Years”. On further reflection, his critique about the American dream of space flight, colonization of outer space, conquering of alien races, and striving to remake the alien into our own image became much clearer. His tone is far more ironic than I first recognized, and his vision of America’s foolhardy confidence and arrogance in colonizing Mars is expertly achieved with parallels to American’s treatment of the American Indians in their colonization of the Western frontier.<br /><br />Here were the standout stories for me:<br /><br />“Ylla” is the opening story and the most impressive. It’s about an older Martian couple in a strained marriage. It’s the only story told entirely from the Martian perspective, and details the tragic events triggered by the arrival of the First Expedition to Mars. It is filled with universal truths and the savagery that can lurk underneath the surface of any relationship. <br /><br />“The Earth Men” is an incredible mix of irony, horror, and humor. The Second Expedition to Mars successfully makes contact with a Martian town’s inhabitants, only to be thoroughly ignored. The Earth Men become increasingly frustrated by the indifference of the Martians, until they are finally granted attention by a particular Martian. However, although he is receptive to their claims of being from Earth, his reasons are far more sinister than anyone could have anticipated. <br /><br />“The Third Expedition” tugs at the nostalgia strings in a very subversive way. The Third Expedition touches down in a different location, hoping to escape the mysterious fate of the first two Expeditions, which were never heard from again. But instead of finding golden-skinned Martians with almond-shaped eyes, then encounter their loved ones from the past impossibly living again in a perfect recreation of their childhood homes. Things are not what they seem, as the Earth men again discover.<br /><br />“—And the Moon Be Still as Bright” and “The Settlers”, in which a member of the Fourth Expedition in entranced by the mysterious beauty of the lost Martian civilization, and disgusted by the crudity of his fellow American astronauts, who cannot appreciate what he says. He turns on them with tragic consequences, saying “we Earth Men have a talent for ruining big, beautiful things”.<br /><br />Unfortunately, the following stories lost a lot of momentum for me. There is a lot of filler material here, and it was hard for me to relate to some of the stories. Although I enjoyed “The Fire Balloons”, “Usher II”, “The Martian”, the other stories that followed were fairly unimpressive. <br /><br />In particular, I thought “The Silent Towns” was a very sexist and insulting depiction of women, as the last man on Mars finds the last woman on Mars after nuclear war on Earth causes Earth Men to leave Mars. Instead of an Adam and Eve story, he discovers the last woman to be overweight, homely, trivial, and all-around pathetic. Bradbury really does seem to have a condescending attitude towards women, and we can’t just excuse that as being a product of the times. The housewives of Fahrenheit 451 are equally contemptuous, and it’s disappointing to see this in such a beloved writer. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10721439480568807169">...more</a>
